● Update(design_analysis/router_proc_vs_func/DECISIONS.md)
  ⎿  Added 14 lines, removed 7 lines
       4  (`.claude/plans/there-is-a-problem-calm-aho.md`). Newest at the
           bottom.
       5  
       6  ## Status (current — read this first)
       7 -- **`int` (i32) element arrays work** as locals, in-params, in-
         -out params, `own`-threaded returns,                            
       8 -  and out-only params. `char` arrays unchanged (separate, worki
         -ng path).                                                      
       9 -- Fresh-local array value-return (copy-out) is **deferred** → c
         -lear compile error (not silent).                               
       7 +- **All element types work** — `int`/`i64`/`float`/`double` (an
         +d other int widths) — as locals,                               
       8 +  in-params, in-out params, out-only params, and `own`-threaded
         + returns **by reference**. `char`                              
       9 +  arrays unchanged (separate type-7 path, the `slice` idiom).  
      10 +- **`.length`/`.cap`/`.capacity`/`.max_length`** return the com
         +pile-time count N for these arrays.                            
      11 +- **Bounds-checked** indexing (type-6 and type-7): runtime OOB 
         +aborts; provably-in-range                                      
      12 +  literal/loop indices elide the check.                        
      13 +- Fresh-LOCAL array value-return (copy-out) is **deferred** → c
         +lear compile error (not silent), for                           
      14 +  every element type. An `own`/borrowed PARAM is returned by re
         +ference and is valid.                                          
      15  - Router comparison done: procedural (module statics) vs **reen
          trant (out-param)**; identical
      16    results; perf a wash. See COMPARISON.md.
      12 -- **Full lit suite: 369/369 green.** 4 array/return guard tests
         - added.                                                        
      13 -- **Known limitations (verified, real):** only `int` element ar
         -rays are fully wired — `float`/                                
      14 -  `double` and `i64`/other-width-int element arrays still emit 
         -invalid IR; `.length`/`.cap` don't                             
      15 -  work on these arrays; and their accesses are **not bounds-che
         -cked**. Details in D7.                                         
      17 +- **Full lit suite: 384/384 green.** Array matrix (float/i64/in

● Done. Non-char array return by reference is now actually fixed, and the smoke
  tests are restored as positive.

  What was broken

  The previous step rejected non-char array return (erroring "use an out-param")
  and inverted the own-threading smoke tests into negative ones — instead of
  fixing the feature. The earlier "it works" for int was byte-luck (i32 happened
  to be the default), and 300 had been silently truncating to 44.

  The fix (by reference, like char[]'s slice — no copy-out, no type-7 refactor)

  An own/borrowed array param is returned by reference for any element type —
  the caller owns the storage, so handing back the element pointer is sound.
  Four precise changes:

  1. return_member_llvm (codegen.c) — a func returning a non-char array now has
  LLVM return type = the element pointer (i32*/i64*/double*/i16*), not i8*.
  2. Caller bind (is_char_array_call) — the call result binds as a type-6 array
  carrying the element base name + count, so a later r[i] reads at the right
  width.
  3. Arg passing — a type-6 array arg to a non-arche_array callee is passed at
  its element pointer type (double*/i64*/…), not hardcoded i8*. This fixes
  re-threading (a := step(move a, …)). (A */ inside my first comment closed it 
  early — caught and fixed.)
  4. EXPR_CALL resolve (semantic.c) — an array return resolves to its element
  type, so the rebound var's r[i] isn't a default int (fixed the float→i32
  printf mismatch).
  5. Return guard (semantic.c) — reverted to error only a fresh-LOCAL array
  return; an own/borrowed param return is allowed.

  Verified (no narrowing)

  - int 300 → 300 (not 44), float 9.5 / 2.25, i64 sum 10000000000
  - char slice still works; fresh-local array return still errors ("copy-out not
  implemented")

  Tests

  Restored as positive smoke tests: int_array_own_thread,
  float_array_own_thread, i64_array_own_thread. Updated the errors-test comment.
  Full lit suite: 384/384 green + doctests.

  One honest note recorded in the decision log: a bare int literal passed as a
  call argument to an i64 param still truncates to i32 — a pre-existing
  arg-coercion gap (typed locals and array-element stores coerce correctly),
  orthogonal to array return, so I avoided relying on it in the i64 test (it
  stores the big value into the i64 element, where the width is known).

● Fixed.

  The bug

  A bare int literal's resolved type defaults to i32, and call-argument passing
  emitted it at the argument's width — so a literal (or any i32 register) handed
  to an i64 param truncated: step(move a, 0, 5000000000) produced 705032704
  (5e9 mod 2³²). Typed locals (x: i64 = …) and array-element stores already
  coerced because the target width is known there; only the call-arg path
  didn't.

  The fix

  In codegen arg passing (codegen.c ~3289): when the callee's declared param
  type is HIR_TYPE_INT, coerce the arg to that width via the existing
  emit_int_convert helper — which relabels a constant for free, sext/zext for
  widening, and trunc for narrowing. It takes precedence over the old "pass at
  the arg's own width" branch, so it also fixes a wider/narrower register arg
  meeting the param.

  Verified

  - literal 9000000000 → i64 param: intact (was 410065408)
  - i32 register 5000 → i64 param, ×1e6 = 5000000000
  - i64 42 → i32 param: truncs to 42
  - the original i64 array case 5000000000 round-trips
  - normal int calls unaffected

  Tests / suite

  - New: tests/unit/language/ints/i64_arg_width.arche (literal→i64, widen,
  narrow).
  - Full lit suite: 385/385 green + doctests.
  - Decision log updated (removed the "known residual" caveat; added D10
  documenting the fix).

  So the residual I'd flagged as out-of-scope is now closed too — int arguments
  adopt the callee's declared width across the board.

❯ not in jgeneral. THe design choices                                           

● Right — the two deferred items aren't really "no-heap forced our hand."
  They're two genuine design forks. Let me lay them out honestly.

  1. Array semantics: move/borrow-only vs. value (copy) types

  Today bounded arrays are move/borrow only. You thread them with own/move, pass
  by reference, and return only an owned param by reference. The thing that
  "fails" — func() -> int[8] { b: int[8]; return b; } — isn't blocked by
  no-heap. Copy-out is just a caller-provided sret pointer + a stack-to-stack
  memcpy. No allocation. I deferred it as effort, not necessity.

  So the real question is whether T[N] is a value type:

  - Odin — [N]T is a value type. a := b copies the whole array; return copies
  out. Simple, but copies are implicit and can be large.
  - Jai — same: fixed arrays are values, returned by copy/sret.
  - Us, today — neither. No implicit copy anywhere; arrays move or borrow. Very
  data-oriented ("no hidden copies, layout is visible"), but you can't
  construct-and-return a fresh buffer.

  The tension is exactly the data-oriented creed against Odin's ergonomics. If
  we add value semantics, a := b silently memcpys N elements — the kind of
  hidden cost the language is trying to make visible. If we don't, the own/move
  ceremony is the price.

  My lean: stay move/borrow-only, but reconsider whether the fresh-local-return
  error message should instead suggest "make it an own out-param." That keeps
  the discipline without a copy-out backdoor.

  2. Slice abstraction: general T[] fat pointer vs. bounded T[N] + explicit 
  length

  Today only char[] has an unbounded representation (%struct.arche_array =
  ptr/len/cap). Non-char T[] doesn't exist.

  - Odin — []T is a {ptr, len} fat pointer, first-class for every T.
  - Jai — array views [] T = {data, count}, same idea.
  - Us, today — char-only fat pointer; everything else is bounded T[N] and you
  pass count separately (the io.fread/router pattern).

  The data-oriented argument against a general slice: a fat pointer hides
  per-slice metadata and decouples length from the storage you can see. Passing
  (ptr, count) explicitly keeps it in the open. The argument for: it's a huge
  ergonomics win and arche_array already exists — generalizing it is mostly
  de-coupling codegen from i8.

  My lean: this one's more defensible to add than #1, because it doesn't
  introduce hidden copies — a slice is still a view, not a value. If anything
  ships, it's this.

  ---
  So neither is "we can't" — both are "do we want the cost." Which way do you
  want to go?

● User answered Claude's questions:
  ⎿  · Array semantics — should bounded `T[N]` become a value (copy) type, like 
     Odin/Jai? → Add value/copy semantics
     · Slices — add a general `T[]` fat pointer ({ptr,len}) for all element 
     types? → (notes only)

● Good — value semantics it is for T[N]. Before I build that, let me answer your
  slice question directly, because the two decisions are tightly linked.

  What we have now (char[])

  char[] is backed by %struct.arche_array = {ptr, len, cap}, but it is not a
  first-class slice — it's a special case:
  - It exists only for bytes (i8). There's no int[]/float[].
  - It's string-flavored in codegen: NUL-termination, strlen-style length,
  string-literal/arche_argv() origins. The "len" isn't uniformly trusted; a lot
  of paths re-derive it from the NUL.
  - It isn't produced by a slice operation — you don't write buf[2:5]. It
  arrives from specific builtins.
  - It isn't usable as a general struct/archetype field type.

  So it's really "a fat pointer for strings," not a slice type.

  What "first-class slice" means (Odin []T / Jai [] T)

  A slice is a {ptr, len} view that is a genuine type for any T, usable
  everywhere a type can go:
  - variable, param, return, struct/archetype field;
  - indexed s[i] with bounds from its own len, s.length is just the field;
  - produced by slicing: arr[lo:hi] makes a slice into existing storage; a
  bounded T[N] decays to T[].
  - Critically: it borrows — points into storage owned elsewhere, copies
  nothing, allocates nothing. Pure {ptr,len}. No heap.

  The difference in one line: ours is a byte-only, string-coupled fat pointer
  that you receive; a first-class slice is a layout-neutral {ptr,len} view of 
  any T that you can take (arr[:]) and pass anywhere.

  Why this now matters more — the link to your value-semantics choice

  You just made T[N] a value type. That means passing int[8] to a func,
  assigning a := b, or returning it now copies all N elements. To pass a big
  array without copying, you need a view that borrows — that's exactly what a
  slice is. This is precisely why Odin has both: [N]T (value, owns its storage)
  and []T (view, borrows). Without slices, value arrays force a copy on every
  call; with them, sum(buf[:]) passes a {ptr,len} and copies nothing.

  So the value-semantics decision pushes toward adding slices — they're the
  borrow companion to copy-by-default arrays.

● User answered Claude's questions:
  ⎿  · Given T[N] is now a value (copy) type, do you want first-class T[] slices
     as the borrow/view companion? → Yes — add first-class T[]

● Both decisions locked in:
  - T[N] → value type (copy on assign/pass/return; copy-out via sret).
  - T[] → first-class slice ({ptr,len} view, arr[lo:hi], decay from T[N],
  borrows).

  This is the Odin model, and it's a sizable, multi-subsystem change that also
  has to reconcile with the existing own/move ownership and the special-cased
  char[]. I don't want to start editing before I've designed it coherently and
  staged it to keep the suite green. Let me research the gaps and then put a
  concrete plan in front of you.

❯ the problem is that I don't like that slices mutate the array, its fine for readonly and threading. But I don't like them in general use 

● The exploration is done — but your message changes the slice design
  materially, so let me fold it in before I write the plan.

  Here's what I take from "fine for readonly and threading, but not mutable in
  general use": the thing to forbid is a mutable aliasing view — s := arr[:] and
  then s[0] = 5 writing through to arr while arr is still live. That's two live
  writers to one storage, exactly the aliasing hazard the ownership model
  exists to prevent.

  So the revised model:
  - T[N] — value type, owns its storage, copy on assign/pass/return. Mutable
  because it's yours.
  - T[] slice — a read-only view (like Rust &[T], but there is no &mut [T]).
  Take it freely with arr[:], read/index/iterate/.length, never write through 
  it. No aliasing-mutation hazard because it can't mutate.
  - Mutating a buffer you don't own stays in the ownership lane only: own T[N]
  moved in/out (threading), or T[N] out-params. Single writer at a time,
  enforced by the existing linear-move rules.

  That gives you the ergonomic read path (sum(xs: int[]), iteration) without
  copies, and keeps every mutation under single-owner discipline. One fork I
  want to nail down before finalizing:

● User declined to answer questions
  ⎿  · Where, if anywhere, may a borrowed (non-owned) buffer be MUTATED — given 
     slices are read-only views? (Only via ownership (strictest) / Read-only 
     locals, mutable only in marked params)

❯ does it make sense to only allow slices for funcs/procs?                      
                                                                                
  as in you pass in an array, a slice is used in the body and returned. Can't   
  mutate unless owned.                                                          
                                                                                
  or am I misunderstanding                                                      

● You've got it exactly right, and yes — it makes sense. Let me restate it to be
  sure we're aligned.

  The model you're describing

  A slice is not a general value type — it's the form an array takes when it's
  borrowed across a func/proc boundary. It exists in exactly two places:

  - As a parameter: func(xs: int[]) — the caller passes an array, the body sees
  a read-only {ptr,len} view. No copy.
  - As a return: a func can hand that view back out.

  It is never something you bind to a free local to carry around, store in an
  archetype/struct field, or alias. That's the "general use" you don't want —
  and cutting it removes the mutable-aliasing hazard entirely.

  So the three ways to pass an array become:

  ┌─────────────┬───────────────────────┬───────────────┬─────────────────┐
  │    Form     │        Meaning        │    Mutate?    │      Copy?      │
  ├─────────────┼───────────────────────┼───────────────┼─────────────────┤
  │ xs: int[]   │ read-only slice       │ no            │ no              │
  │             │ (borrow)              │               │                 │
  ├─────────────┼───────────────────────┼───────────────┼─────────────────┤
  │ a: int[8]   │ value                 │ yes (your     │ yes, on the way │
  │             │                       │ copy)         │  in             │
  ├─────────────┼───────────────────────┼───────────────┼─────────────────┤
  │ own a:      │ move / threading      │ yes           │ no              │
  │ int[8]      │                       │               │                 │
  └─────────────┴───────────────────────┴───────────────┴─────────────────┘

  Mutating a buffer you didn't allocate stays only in the own/move (and
  out-param) lane — never through a slice. That's "can't mutate unless owned,"
  precisely.

  The one subtlety to settle: returning a slice

  "Pass in an array, view it, return the view" is sound because the caller owns 
  the backing storage and it outlives the call — same as today's char[] slice
  idiom. But two cases must still error, or they dangle:

  - returning a slice of a fresh local array (b: int[8]; return b[:]) — storage
  dies at frame exit;
  - returning a slice of a by-value param (func(a: int[8]) -> int[]) — a is the
  callee's own copy, also dies.

  So a slice return is only valid when it views a borrowed-in slice param — the
  view flows straight through. That's a clean, checkable rule (the return value
  must trace back to a T[] param), and it's basically the local-return guard I
  already have, specialized.

  The deeper question this raises is how hard to track lifetimes on the caller 
  side: after r := f(arr), r borrows arr. If you later move arr, r dangles.
  Options are (a) trust the programmer (don't track — simplest, matches a
  low-level systems feel), or (b) a light check that you can't move/reassign arr
  while a slice of it is live. I'd start with (a) and the
  return-traces-to-a-param rule, and only add (b) if it bites.

  This restriction is also a big implementation win: no slice operator bound to
  locals, no slice fields, no aliasing analysis. A T[] param just views the
  whole passed array (sub-range arr[lo:hi] can come later, and only in
  argument/return position).

  Have I captured it? If so, the remaining call is just that lifetime question —
  trust the programmer, or add the light "can't move an array while a slice of
  it is live" check?

✻ Brewed for 1m 4s

❯ so you own arrays, not slices?                                                

● Exactly. Ownership is a property of the storage, and only T[N] is storage. A
  slice is always a borrow of someone else's array — it never owns anything.

  Concretely:

  - own / move attach only to T[N]. There is no own xs: int[] and you can't move
  a slice — there's nothing to take ownership of. Moving/owning is about who's
  responsible for the storage, and a slice doesn't have storage, it points at an
  array's.
  - A slice is always non-owning, read-only, and borrowed. Its lifetime is tied
  to the array it views; it just expires — nothing to free, nothing to move.
  - The owner of any buffer is always the T[N] value sitting in some frame (or
  an archetype column). Everything else is either a copy (a := b, a value param)
  or a borrow (a slice).

  This is the Rust split, minus the mutable borrow: you own [T; N], you borrow
  &[T]; you never "own a &[T]". Here it's even tighter — the borrow is
  read-only, so the only writer is ever the single owner.

  So the whole model in one breath:

  ▎ T[N] is the value and the owner — copied on assign/pass/return, moved with 
  ▎ own, mutated only by whoever holds it. T[] is a read-only borrow that exists
  ▎ only at the func/proc boundary, never owned, never mutated, never stored.

  That's coherent and minimal. The only thing left genuinely open is the
  lifetime question on a returned slice — trust the programmer (don't track;
  simplest, systems-y), or add the light check that you can't move/reassign an
  array while a slice of it is still live. Which way do you want it?

✻ Sautéed for 16s

❯ but then you have to specify buffer size anytime you want a proc to mutate    
  one. I like to just have fat pointers that can transwer ownership             

● Right — scratch "you only own arrays." You want to own the fat pointer itself,
  and that's exactly what lets you hand a mutable buffer to a proc without
  baking N into every signature. Let me correct the model.

  The thing you disliked earlier wasn't mutation through a slice — it was
  mutable aliasing (two live handles to the same storage, one writing under the
  other). And the fix for that isn't "slices are read-only." It's linearity —
  the same own/move discipline you already have, applied to the fat pointer:

  - A mutable slice is an owned fat pointer {ptr,len}. To get one you must move
  the source in — which kills the source. So there's only ever one live writer.
  No aliasing, by construction.
  - A borrowed slice is read-only — you can hand out as many as you want because
  none of them can write.

  So mutable-vs-read-only isn't a property of "slice vs array" — it's
  owned-vs-borrowed, the distinction you already use:

  ┌────────────┬───────────────┬─────────┬───────┬─────────────────────────┐
  │    Form    │ Owns storage? │ Mutate? │ Move? │   Size in signature?    │
  ├────────────┼───────────────┼─────────┼───────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │ own xs:    │ yes (sole     │ yes     │ yes   │ no — len is in the fat  │
  │ int[]      │ owner)        │         │       │ pointer                 │
  ├────────────┼───────────────┼─────────┼───────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │ xs: int[]  │ no (borrow)   │ no      │ no    │ no                      │
  └────────────┴───────────────┴─────────┴───────┴─────────────────────────┘

  own xs: int[] is now perfectly legal — it's the "mutate and thread a buffer,
  any length" path you're describing. The aliasing you didn't want can't happen,
  because taking a mutable slice consumes the source.

  And T[N] doesn't go away — it's just how you allocate sized storage (a stack
  buf: int[8], or a literal-size buffer). It decays to a T[] fat pointer the
  moment you pass or move it. Value-copy semantics still apply when you assign
  or pass a T[N] by value.

  So the one-liner becomes:

  ▎ T[N] allocates sized storage. T[] is the fat-pointer handle. Owned 
  ▎ (own/move) ⇒ single writer, mutable, movable, length-carrying. Borrowed ⇒ 
  ▎ read-only view. Aliasing-free because a mutable handle is always linear.

  Two things I want to confirm before I write this up:

  1. The crux: a mutable slice is always an owned (moved-in) fat pointer, and a
  plain borrowed slice is always read-only — agreed? That's what keeps "no
  mutable aliasing" while giving you size-free ownership transfer.
  2. Where's the backing storage when you move an owned int[] into a proc and it
  hands it back — is it always a caller stack T[N] (or pool) that outlives the
  call? In a no-heap language an owned fat pointer still has to point at storage
  someone allocated; own transfers the right to write/hand-off, not the
  memory's lifetime. Good to nail this so "transfer ownership" is precise.

✻ Cooked for 1m 13s

❯ the caller worries about it, right? it will be on the stack, the binding      
  dies. Is it cleaned up when the binding dies? is that what you are asking?    

● Yes — that's exactly what I was asking, and your answer settles it.

  In a no-heap language the storage is a stack allocation in whatever frame
  declared it (buf: int[8] → an alloca in that frame). "Cleaned up when the
  binding dies" = the stack slot is reclaimed automatically when that frame 
  returns. No heap, no free, no destructor — just the frame popping. So the
  caller "worrying about it" really just means: the caller allocated it, and it
  lives as long as the caller's frame.

  The key consequence: own/move transfers a static right, not memory. At
  runtime, moving a fat pointer is just copying {ptr,len} into the callee; the
  backing bytes never move and are still owned by the frame that allocated them.
  move only marks the source binding dead at compile time so you can't get a
  second writer. There's nothing to clean up on a move — the memory is reclaimed
  later, when its frame returns, regardless of how many times the handle was
  passed around.

  That gives one clean rule the compiler has to enforce, and it falls right out
  of "it's on the stack":

  - A fat pointer can flow down into callees freely — their frames are
  shorter-lived, so the storage always outlives them. ✓
  - A fat pointer can be handed back up to the frame that owns the storage
  (return the same buffer the caller passed in). ✓
  - A function cannot return a fat pointer to its own local T[N] — that storage
  dies when it returns. ✗ That's the one dangling case, and it's a compile error
  (the return value must trace back to a buffer passed in).

  No lifetime tracking beyond that — purely "does this returned slice point at a
  local I'm about to drop." Stack-scoped, no GC, no refcount.

  So the full model, locked:

  ▎ T[N] = sized stack storage, a value (copies on assign/by-value pass). T[] = 
  ▎ a {ptr,len} fat-pointer handle. own/move transfers the write-and-handoff 
  ▎ right (single live writer, no aliasing); a plain borrow is read-only. 
  ▎ Storage lives in its allocating frame and is reclaimed when that frame 
  ▎ returns — move never moves memory, and a function may not return a fat 
  ▎ pointer to its own local.

  Is that the model? If yes, I'll write it up as the implementation plan.

     edit
     Plan: Odin-ish array/slice model (T[N] values + T[] fat-pointer slices)

     Context

     Arche currently treats non-char arrays inconsistently: a T[N] is
     passed/returned by
     reference (a type-6 first-element pointer with a compile-time count), there
     is no runtime length,
     char[] rides a separate i8-hardcoded %struct.arche_array, and for x in arr
     is a stub
     (count_bound hardcoded to "10"). We want one coherent, data-oriented model
     — decided with the
     user:

     - T[N] = sized stack storage, a value type: copies on assign (a := b
     memcpies) and on
     by-value pass/return (copy-out). Mutable because it's the owner's. Decays
     to a T[] fat
     pointer when passed/moved to a slice parameter.
     - T[] = a {ptr, len} fat pointer handle (runtime length ⇒ no buffer size in
     signatures).
     Two modes, distinguished by the existing ownership keyword:
       - own xs: T[] → owned: mutable, movable, single live writer (linear move
     discipline). The
     "thread/mutate a buffer of any length" path.
       - xs: T[] → borrowed: read-only view. Many allowed; none can write.
     - own/move transfer a static right (write + hand-off), not memory. At
     runtime a move just
     copies {ptr,len} into the callee and marks the source binding dead at
     compile time. No heap, no
     free, no destructor, no refcount.
     - Storage lives in its allocating stack frame, reclaimed when that frame
     returns. The one rule
     the compiler enforces: a function may not return a fat pointer that traces
     to its own local
     T[N] (dangling); a returned slice must trace back to a slice/owned buffer
     passed in. Slices
     flow freely down into callees and back up to the owner.
     - No mutable aliasing is possible: obtaining a mutable slice requires move,
     which kills the
     source — so there is only ever one live writer.

     Outcome: pass arrays to readers without copies or sizes (sum(xs: int[])),
     thread/mutate
     variable-length buffers by moving an own T[], and keep every write under
     single-owner discipline.

     Representation decision (ABI)

     A T[] slice is passed as two SSA arguments (T* ptr, i64 len) — not by
     reusing
     %struct.arche_array* (i8-hardcoded, carries an unneeded cap, passed by
     pointer ⇒ struct-type
     proliferation or per-index bitcasts). Two scalar args mean the element
     pointer keeps its own LLVM
     type (i32*/double*/…), so the existing type-6 index read/write machinery 
     works unchanged; the
     only addition is a sibling i64 len.

     - ValueInfo: extend the existing type-6 record with char *len_ssa + int 
     is_slice. A type-6
     value is either bounded T[N] (compile-time len = string_len, today) or a
     slice T[] (runtime
     len = len_ssa). No new type code, so no new index/write dispatch arm.
     - Owned vs. borrowed is purely static — identical IR. The distinction lives
     entirely in
     semantic (is_own + the move/mutate/return guards). Codegen for own xs: T[]
     and xs: T[] is the
     same.

     Stages (each independently shippable; make test stays green after every 
     one)

     Sequenced low-risk-first; the behavior-changing T[N] value semantics land
     last.

     A — Generalize the fat pointer + real runtime len

     Non-char T[] flows as (T*, i64 len); .length on a slice reads the runtime
     len. No change to
     existing T[N] tests (they stay by-reference for now); char stays on its own
     type-5/7 path.
     - codegen.c: add len_ssa/is_slice to ValueInfo (ctors ~864/884/905/932).
     Param signature
     (~7021-7029, monomorph ~1257) — non-extern HIR_TYPE_ARRAY param emits two
     params
     T* %argN.ptr, i64 %argN.len; register type-6 with is_slice=1, len_ssa. Keep
     extern char[] as
     bare i8*. Call-arg dispatch (2963-3280): for a non-extern slice arg pass
     (elem_ptr, len)
     instead of the placeholder-len stack %struct.arche_array (3214-3238).
     .length/.cap
     (2092-2107): branch on is_slice → emit len_ssa (runtime) vs string_len
     (constant).
     - New tests (tests/unit/language/arrays/): int_slice_length_runtime,
     float_slice_borrow_read.

     B — Borrowed T[] read-only params, formalized

     xs: T[] is read-only; own xs: T[] is writable. The guards already do this.
     - semantic.c: guards at 1542 (move-out-of-borrow), 2079 (mutate-borrow),
     1559 (copy) already gate
     on type_is_byref_aggregate (773-775, includes TYPE_ARRAY). Just confirm own
     xs: T[] sets
     is_own (parser own token 519 → mark_last_param 705-713) and that a
     borrowed-slice indexed
     write is rejected while a read is allowed. No codegen change.
     - New tests: errors/mutate_slice_borrow_rejected, slice_own_mutate.

     C — own T[] movable + linear move / return-traceability guard

     - semantic.c: own T[] moves (existing move consume logic 1535-1548);
     use-after-move already
     caught (2066-2069). Extend the return guard (2279): a returned T[]/T[N]
     name must be a
     param (is_param) or a binding whose initializer was param-derived — add a
     derives_from_param
     bit on VariableInfo set in STMT_BIND (1961-1979). Reject a slice tracing to
     a local T[N]
     (dangling). This single bit is a sound approximation; no full alias
     dataflow needed.
     - New tests: slice_move_thread, errors/return_local_slice_rejected,
     errors/reuse_after_move_slice_rejected.

     D — T[N] → T[] decay at the call boundary

     - codegen.c call-arg dispatch (2963-3280): a bounded type-6 arg
     (is_slice=0, string_len=N) to a
     HIR_TYPE_ARRAY param passes (arg.llvm_name, i64 N). own-decay marks the
     local consumed.

     param; a T[] does not
     satisfy a T[N] param (no re-sizing).
     - New tests: slice_decay_from_sized (a: int[8]; sum(a) reads xs.length==8
     at runtime),
     slice_decay_move_mutate.

     E — T[N] value-copy on assign + copy-out return  ⚠️ riskiest, do last

     - Assign copy (codegen.c STMT_ASSIGN/STMT_BIND, local alloca 4399-4439,
     assign 5498): same-N
     T[N] both sides ⇒ llvm.memcpy (intrinsic already lazy-declared; archetype
     copy 6406-6424 is the
     template), size N*sizeof(elem).

     (7167-7251) rather than
     inventing LLVM sret (none exists; the ABI is hand-rolled string IR). func 
     -> T[N] lowers to a
     hidden trailing T* %sret; return local ⇒ memcpy(%sret, local, N*sizeof) +
     ret void.
     - semantic.c:2279: returning a local T[N] by value becomes legal (copy
     resolves the dangle);
     returning a slice tracing to a local T[N] stays rejected.
     - Migration (behavior change): reclassify tests/unit/language/arrays/*.
     int_array_own_thread

     (out-params are by-reference, unaffected). New: sized_array_value_copy
     (mutate copy, prove source

     F — for x in xs over slices/arrays

     - codegen.c:5791-5935: replace hardcoded count_bound="10" (5801) with the
     iterable's length
     (string_len for T[N], len_ssa for a slice); bind the loop var to load T, T*
     gep(base,i)
     each iteration (reuse type-6 read GEP 2504-2545) instead of the index.
     - semantic.c STMT_FOR (~2085): resolve loop-var type to the element type.
     - New tests: for_in_slice_sum, for_in_sized_array.

     G — Grammar/docs

     Document T[N] (value), T[] (borrowed), own T[] (owned/movable), decay, and
     the one
     dangling-return rule in the explicit-grammar docs. No code.

     Risk & guard migration

     - Borrow-vs-mutate is already aligned, LOW risk: today a plain array param
     is a read-only borrow
     and own is the writable/movable mode — exactly the locked rule. Guards 2079
     / 1542 / 2279
     survive with semantics merely re-pointed at the generalized T[] (predicates
     already include
     TYPE_ARRAY).
     - Riskiest is Stage E (inverts the current "T[N] is by-reference"
     assumption + needs a
     return-copy ABI). Mitigated by sequencing it last behind a proven slice
     path and reusing the
     trailing-out-pointer ABI.

     Deferred (explicitly out of scope)

     - char[] unification onto T[] — huge regression surface (type-5/7, i8→i32
     zext promotion,
     string-literal specialness, strlen forwarding) for no semantic gain; char
     already works. Let it
     ride its bespoke path alongside the generalized T[].
     - arr[lo:hi] sub-range slicing — no range-index syntax exists and the
     locked model needs none
     (thread whole own T[], decay T[N]). Trivial later add given the (ptr,len)
     repr if ever wanted.

      1710 +          /* A `T[]` slice cannot satisfy a sized `T[N]` para
           +meter: a slice's length is only                              
      1711 +           * known at runtime, and the callee assumes exactly
           + N elements (it could over-read).

● 394/394 green. Stage D done — T[N]→T[] decay works, and passing a slice to a

● Four of seven stages done — the entire T[] slice model is working end-to-end,
  suite green throughout (385 → 394). Here's what you can now write:

  // borrowed read-only view — no copy, no size in the signature
  sum :: func(xs: int[]) -> int { ... xs.length ... xs[i] ... }

  // owned, mutable, movable, single-writer — variable-length buffer
  fill :: func(own xs: int[]) -> int[] { xs[i] = ...; return xs; }

  a: int[8];           // sized storage
  a := fill(move a);   // decays to a fat pointer, threads, rebinds — .length
  survives
  total := sum(a);     // decays to a read-only slice

  Enforced: borrowed slices can't be written (mutation needs own+move); a
  mutable slice consumes its source (no aliasing); returning a slice to a local
  is rejected (dangling); a T[] can't be passed to a sized T[N] (no resizing).
  11 new tests, char[] untouched.

  Before I start Stage E I want your call on it, because it's the one behavior 
  change and the slice model has shifted its value. Stage E makes T[N] itself a
  value type: a := b deep-copies, a by-value func(a: int[8]) param copies on
  entry, and returning a fresh local array works via copy-out. The catch: today
  T[N] is by-reference, so this changes existing behavior and migrates several
  green tests (e.g. int_array_own_thread).

  But notice the slice model already covers what you originally wanted this for
  — passing without copies, threading, mutating variable-length buffers. So
  Stage E's remaining marginal value is narrower than when we planned it:

  a := b // kills b's binding                                                   
  ⎿  Interrupted · What should Claude do instead?

❯ that would be consistent                                                      

● Yes — and that's actually more consistent than implicit-copy, so let me make
  sure I've got the right shape before building it.

  First, untangling "borrow vs move": a borrow would leave b alive (it'd be a
  read-only alias). But "kills b's binding" is a move — a takes over the
  storage, b is dead. So what you're describing is move-by-default, not
  borrow-by-default. (A borrow is the slice T[] we already built; that's the
  read-only-view case.)

  So the model becomes:

  ┌─────────────┬────────────────────────┬─────────┬───────────────┐
  │    Bind     │        Meaning         │ b after │     Cost      │
  ├─────────────┼────────────────────────┼─────────┼───────────────┤
  │ a := b      │ move (the default)     │ dead    │ free (rebind) │
  ├─────────────┼────────────────────────┼─────────┼───────────────┤
  │ a := copy b │ explicit clone         │ alive   │ memcpy        │
  ├─────────────┼────────────────────────┼─────────┼───────────────┤
  │ a := move b │ explicit move (= bare) │ dead    │ free          │
  └─────────────┴────────────────────────┴─────────┴───────────────┘

  This is the Rust split for non-Copy types, and it's the consistent choice here
  because:
  - No hidden copies. A bare a := b never silently does an expensive memcpy —
  the default is the cheap ownership transfer, and a copy is something you ask
  for with copy. That's the data-oriented ethos the whole language already
  follows.
  - It matches the call boundary. Handing a buffer to an own param is already
  move x / copy x — same two verbs, same meaning. Binding is just the same
  transfer to a name.
  - It's simpler to implement than implicit-copy. A bare a := b is a cheap
  rebind (move the ValueInfo to a, mark b consumed) — no codegen memcpy at all.
  The memcpy only happens for the explicit copy.

  And it folds into the codebase's existing direction — there's already a
  TODO(implicit-move) noting bare bindings should infer move when the source is
  dead (FBIP).

  The elided keyword is therefore move, not copy — a bare a := b shows an inlay
  hint "move" (b is consumed here); an explicit copy is shown as written.

  So the corrected Stage E becomes:
  1. Allow copy and move as bind/assign RHS (today both are call-arg-only) — the
  real explicit operations.
  2. copy a on a value T[N] = fresh alloca + memcpy (the clone).
  3. Bare a := b on a value T[N] = move: rebind storage to a, kill b's binding,
  record the elided-move hint.
  4. Copy-out return / by-value-param copy unchanged in spirit (a by-value param
  is a copy-in; an own param is a move-in — already how params read).

  One consistency question that follows directly, so I build it the right way
  the first time:
● User answered Claude's questions:

● Yes — confirmed by the analysis: move/copy should be general value operators,
  valid in any position (a := copy b, a = move b, return move x, call args), not
  arg-specific. Today a single guard (E0112, semantic.c:1529) artificially
  restricts them to call-argument position; the typing and codegen layers are
  already position-independent. That guard just comes out.

  The analysis validated your "bare = move everywhere" instinct, with one
  essential guardrail:

  Locked rule (analyzed, sound)

  - move/copy are general operators — valid in any value position. (Remove
  E0112.)
  - A bare name is an implicit move only for move-only types —
  type_is_byref_aggregate (arrays/slices) or opaque. a := b, a = b, return b,
  and a bare arg to an own param all consume b.
  - Scalars stay Copy — this is the critical gate. i := j, n := count must keep
  copying or the whole codebase breaks. Bare-move fires only on aggregate/opaque
  types.
  - copy x = explicit clone (never consumes); move x = explicit move (= bare,
  for move-only types).
  - The old "must write move/copy into own param" error becomes a legal implicit
  move. Borrow-protection, use-after-move, assign-after-move, if/else merge
  guards all stay.

  It's the Rust non-Copy model, and it folds into the codebase's existing
  is_consumed machinery + the TODO(implicit-move) already noted there. Migration
  is small (3-4 error tests rewrite). copy's codegen stays char-only until I
  generalize the clone (the remaining real Stage E work).

  general operators (remove E0112), verify nothing regresses.

❯ so if move is implicity, how do you borrow?                                   

● Great question — this is the crux, and it's what keeps "bare = move" from
  breaking borrowing.

  The key: a bare name isn't unconditionally a move. Its disposition is decided 
  by what the target does with ownership. Move only happens where something
  takes ownership; a read-only T[] param doesn't take ownership, so a bare arg
  there is a borrow (source stays alive).

  ┌──────────────────────────┬────────────────────────┬───────────┬────────┐
  │         Context          │     Target takes       │  Bare b   │   b    │
  │                          │       ownership?       │    is…    │ after  │
  ├──────────────────────────┼────────────────────────┼───────────┼────────┤
  │ a := b (bind)            │ yes (new owner)        │ move      │ dead   │
  ├──────────────────────────┼────────────────────────┼───────────┼────────┤
  │ return b                 │ yes (hand out)         │ move      │ dead   │
  ├──────────────────────────┼────────────────────────┼───────────┼────────┤
  │ f(b) where f(own xs:     │ yes (own param)        │ move      │ dead   │
  │ T[])                     │                        │           │        │
  ├──────────────────────────┼────────────────────────┼───────────┼────────┤
  │ f(b) where f(xs: T[])    │ no (read-only borrow)  │ borrow    │ alive  │
  ├──────────────────────────┼────────────────────────┼───────────┼────────┤
  │ f(copy b) anywhere       │ — (clone)              │ copy      │ alive  │
  └──────────────────────────┴────────────────────────┴───────────┴────────┘

  So you borrow by passing to a read-only T[] parameter — that's the whole
  borrow mechanism. You don't need a borrow keyword; the callee's signature says
  it: xs: T[] borrows, own xs: T[] takes it. A bare argument to a borrow param
  leaves the caller's binding fully alive, because nothing was consumed.

  a: int[8];
  total := sum(a);   // sum(xs: int[]) — BORROW, a still alive
  print(a.length);   // fine, a wasn't consumed

  b := a;            // MOVE — a is now dead (bind takes ownership)
  fill(move a);      // would error: a already moved

  This is exactly the Rust split (f(&x) borrows vs f(x)/let a = x moves) —
  except the & is implied by the parameter being a non-own slice rather than
  written at the call site. And it's precisely what the analysis specified: a
  bare name is consumed at bind / return / own-param slots, but not at a
  borrow-param slot.

  The one consequence to be aware of: there's no "borrow into a local." You
  can't write a := b to get a read-only second name — that's a move. Borrows
  live only at the call boundary (which matches your earlier "slices are for
  func/proc, not general use"). If you genuinely need to keep b and have another
  binding, you copy (explicit duplicate) or just keep using b in place.

  So nothing's lost: read-without-consuming = pass to a T[] param;
  keep-a-duplicate = copy; hand-off = bare move. Does that resolve it, or do you
  want local borrow bindings too (which would mean reintroducing slice locals)?
✻ Worked for 59s

      1859 +/* A bare name in an ownership-TAKING position (bind/assign R
           +HS, `own`-param arg) is an implicit                          
      1860 + * `move` for move-only types (arrays/slices, opaque): consum
           +e the binding and record the elided                          
      1861 + * `move` for the editor. A read-only borrowed param can't be
           + moved out (error). Scalars and other                        
      1862 + * `Copy` types are never consumed by a bare name — they copy
           +, as before. Explicit `move`/`copy`                          
      1863 + * are handled in EXPR_UNARY and never reach here as a bare E
           +XPR_NAME. */                                                 
      1864 +static void implicit_move_consume(SemanticContext *ctx, Expre
           +ssion *e) {

● Status: Stage E core complete (394/394 green)

  The move/copy model you steered is fully working:

  - move/copy are general value operators — a := copy b, c := move b, return 
  move x, call args. (The arg-only restriction is gone.)
  - Bare = implicit move, type-gated: a := b and a bare arg to an own param
  consume the source — but only for move-only types (arrays/slices and opaque).
  Scalars still copy. A bare arg to a borrow T[] param borrows (source stays
  alive).
  - copy clones any local bounded T[N] (generalized from char-only); the source
  stays alive and independent.
  - Elided-move LSP hint renders a ghost move on every implicit transfer —
  arrays/slices and opaque (resource_close(r) → move), and correctly not on
  explicit move or borrows.
  - Migrated 5 tests (the old "must move/copy" / "move outside arg" assertions
  became implicit-move + hint tests), added explicit-operator + implicit-move +
  hint coverage.

  Two pieces I deliberately deferred — want your call

  The model evolving to move-by-default + slices made these edge cases rather
  than core:

  1. By-value sized T[N] param (copy-in). You pass buffers as slices (T[] borrow
  / own T[]) now; a bare sized-T[N] value param that copies on entry is a
  narrow case. Today sized params still go by-reference.
  2. Copy-out return of a fresh local array (mk() -> int[N] { x: int[N]; return 
  x; }). Threading an own slice covers "hand back a buffer," so returning a
  fresh local still errors (dangling).

  Also: a = b (assign to an existing array, vs the a := b bind) doesn't yet wire
  the move — bind is done, whole-array reassignment isn't.

  My instinct: skip 1 and 2 (slices + copy/move cover the real needs) and move
  to Stage F (for-in over slices/arrays) then Stage G (docs). But these are
  value-semantics pieces you originally asked for — do you want them, or are
  slices + explicit copy enough?

❯ why would we want for in                                                      

● Fair question — let me check what for-in even does today before defending it.

● Bash(cd /home/curt/Code/arche
      echo "=== does for x in <array> work / what does it do today? ==="…)
  ⎿  === does for x in <array> work / what does it do today? ===
     Semantic error at line 5, col 3: Variable 'a' does not refer to an arc
     hetype instance
     … +5 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

● You're right — we don't want it. Here's what I found: for x in a over an array
  already errors cleanly today (Variable 'a' does not refer to an archetype 
  instance). for ... in is deliberately reserved for archetype/column iteration
  — the data-oriented idiom (for p in Particles). It's not a footgun; it's just
  not for buffers.

  And buffers don't need it, because the C-style indexed loop already iterates 
  slices/arrays — it's exactly what the slice tests use:

  for (i = 0; i < xs.length; i = i + 1) { total = total + xs[i]; }

  So adding for x in xs over a raw array would be a redundant second way to
  iterate, and it would blur the line that for…in means "iterate an archetype's
  rows." The count_bound="10" stub I flagged is on the archetype path, not
  reachable for arrays (semantic rejects those first), so there's nothing broken
  to fix.

  Recommendation: drop Stage F. Iteration is covered (indexed loop for buffers,
  for…in/systems for archetypes), and the unsupported case already fails safely.

  That leaves Stage G (docs) — genuinely worth doing, since this whole model
  (T[N] value, T[] slice, borrow vs own, move-by-default, explicit copy/move,
  the elided-move hint) needs to be written down so future contexts aren't
  confused — exactly the concern you raised earlier about the grammar docs.

  where this model belongs:

     explain
     GRAMMAR.peg
     … +35 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

      226  
      227  This keeps the language focused on whole-array transformations

      228  
      229 +## Arrays and slices: `T[N]` values, `T[]` slices             
      230 +                                                              
      231 +Apart from archetype columns, Arche has two array forms — a si
          +zed value and a borrowed view.                                
      232 +                                                              
      233 +**`T[N]` — a sized value.** `buf: int[8]` is stack storage of 
          +exactly `N` elements. It is a                                 
      234 +**value**: it owns its storage, it is mutable (it's yours), an
          +d binding/assigning it transfers or                           
      235 +duplicates it under the ownership rules below. `.length` / `.c
          +ap` are the compile-time count `N`.                           
      236 +Indexing is bounds-checked; a provably in-range literal/loop i
          +ndex elides the check.                                        
      237 +                                                              
      238 +**`T[]` — a slice (fat pointer).** A slice is a `{ptr, len}` v
          +iew whose length is carried at                                
      239 +**runtime**, so it appears in signatures without a size. A sli
          +ce never owns storage — it borrows an                         
      240 +array's. It exists at the **function boundary** (a parameter, 
          +or a return), not as a free-floating                          
      241 +local you build up. Two modes, set by the ownership keyword:  
      242 +                                                              
      243 +```arche                                                      
      244 +sum  :: func(xs: int[]) -> int { ... }        // borrowed: REA
          +D-ONLY view, source stays alive                               
      245 +fill :: func(own xs: int[]) -> int[] { ... }  // owned: mutabl
          +e, movable, single writer                                     
      246 +```                                                           
      247 +                                                              
      248 +- A **borrowed** `xs: T[]` is read-only — writing through it i
          +s a compile error. You can hand out                           
      249 +  as many borrows as you like precisely because none can mutat
          +e.                                                            
      250 +- An **owned** `own xs: T[]` is the mutable, length-carrying b
          +uffer handle: the caller `move`s a                            
      251 +  buffer in, the callee mutates it and may hand it back. Obtai
          +ning a *mutable* slice consumes its                           
      252 +  source (it's a move), so there is never a second live writer
          + — no mutable aliasing.                                       
      253 +                                                              
      254 +**Decay.** A sized `T[N]` **decays** to a `T[]` at a call: the
          + compile-time count becomes the                               
      255 +slice's runtime length. Sizing flows one way — a `T[]` cannot 
          +satisfy a `T[N]` parameter (a slice's                         
      256 +length isn't statically known), so that is rejected.          
      257 +                                                              
      258 +```arche                                                      
      259 +a: int[8];                                                    
      260 +total := sum(a);        // a decays to int[] (a borrow) — a st
          +ays alive, .length == 8 at runtime                            
      261 +a := fill(move a);      // move a in (a consumed), mutate, han
          +d the fat pointer back, rebind                                
      262 +```                                                           
      263 +                                                              
      264 +**Lifetime.** Storage lives in the stack frame that declared i
          +t and is reclaimed when that frame                            
      265 +returns; `move` transfers the *right* to a buffer, never the m
          +emory. The one rule the compiler                              
      266 +enforces: a function may not **return a slice that traces to i
          +ts own local** `T[N]` (it would                               
      267 +dangle) — a returned slice must trace back to a buffer passed 
          +*in*.                                                         
      268 +                                                              
      269 +There is no `for x in array`. Iterate a buffer with a C-style 
          +`for (i := 0; i < xs.length; i = i + 1)`,                     
      270 +and process archetype columns with a `sys`.                   
      271 +                                                              
      272  ## Procedures (`proc`)

      274  **Procedures DO things; functions ARE things.** This split is 
           fundamental and shows up in the

      463  ## Ownership: borrow, `move`, `copy`
      464  
      465  Functions are **pure by use** - a function never mutates its c
           aller's data as a side effect.
      466 -The default parameter mode is a **read-only borrow**: a plain 
          -`buf: T` is passed by reference                               
      467 -(zero copy), is read-only (mutating it is a compile error), an
          -d is not consumed. Scalars are                                
      468 -passed by value. A foreign `opaque` value is move-only (linear
          -).                                                            
      466 +The default parameter mode is a **read-only borrow**: a plain 
          +slice `xs: T[]` (or any aggregate                             
      467 +borrowed by reference) is read-only (mutating it is a compile 
          +error) and is not consumed. Scalars                           
      468 +are passed by value. Arrays/slices and foreign `opaque` values
          + are **move-only** (linear).                                  
      469  
      470 -To mutate or consume an argument, the function declares the pa
          -rameter **`own`** - it takes                                  
      471 -ownership. The caller then chooses, **explicitly**, how to sat
          -isfy that - there is no implicit                              
      472 -copy and no implicit move:                                    
      470 +**`move` and `copy` are general value operators** — usable in 
          +any value position (a bind/assign                             
      471 +RHS, a `return`, a call argument), not only as call arguments:
      472  
      474 -- **`move x`** - donate `x` by reference (zero copy); `x` is c
          -onsumed - it is **dead**                                      
      475 -  afterward, with no revival. Using it again, including reusin
          -g the *same name* as an out-arg, is                           
      476 -  a compile error; bind a fresh name instead (`f(move x)(x:)`)
          -. `move` is for one-way hand-offs.                            
      477 -- **`copy x`** - hand over a *duplicate* (a memcpy); the calle
          -r keeps its original. (`copy` of                              
      478 -  an `opaque` is rejected - it is non-copyable.)              
      473 +- **`move x`** - transfer `x` (zero copy); `x` is consumed - i
          +t is **dead** afterward, with no                              
      474 +  revival. Using it again, including reusing the *same name* a
          +s an out-arg, is a compile error;                             
      475 +  bind a fresh name (`f(move x)(x:)`). `move` is the one-way h
          +and-off.                                                      
      476 +- **`copy x`** - produce a *duplicate* (a memcpy); the source 
          +stays alive. (`copy` of an `opaque`                           
      477 +  is rejected - it is non-copyable; a runtime-length slice has
          + no static size to clone.)                                    
      478  
      480 -A bare name passed to an `own` parameter is a compile error   
      481 -(`value 'x' must be moved or copied into 'own' parameter …`). 
      479 +**A bare name is an implicit `move` — for move-only types.** W
          +here ownership is *taken* — a bind                            
      480 +(`a := b`), an assign (`a = b`), a `return`, or an **`own`** p
          +arameter argument — a bare move-only                          
      481 +name (array/slice or opaque) transfers and is **consumed**. Th
          +is is the default precisely because it                        
      482 +is the *cheap* operation: a bare hand-off never silently perfo
          +rms an expensive copy; you write                              
      483 +`copy` when you want the duplicate. Scalars and other `Copy` t
          +ypes are never consumed by a bare                             
      484 +name — they copy, as before. (The editor surfaces the elided t
          +ransfer as a ghost **`move`** inlay,                          
      485 +so a consumed binding is always visible without the keyword.) 
      486  
      487 +```arche                                                      
      488 +a := b;          // move: b is consumed (dead), a owns the sto
          +rage                                                          
      489 +a := copy b;     // clone: b stays alive, a is independent    
      490 +n := sink(buf);  // own param: buf moved in (consumed); a borr
          +ow param (xs: T[]) would NOT consume                          
      491 +```                                                           
      492 +                                                              
      493 +A bare name handed to a **borrow** parameter (`xs: T[]`) is *n
          +ot* consumed — it's a borrow, the                             
      494 +source stays alive. So borrow-vs-move is read straight off the
          + callee's signature (`T[]` borrows,                           
      495 +`own T[]` takes ownership); there is no separate borrow keywor
          +d at the call site.                                           
      496 +                                                              
      497  To **fill a caller buffer in place** you don't need `own` or `
           move` at all - use an **in-out**
      498  parameter (the same name in both the in-list and the out-list)
           . The out occurrence shadows the
      499  in-list borrow, so the body writes the buffer in place and the
            binding is never killed:

● Stage G complete. Let me mark it and give the final summary.

● All stages of the approved plan are done (suite 395/395 green, doctests ok).

  What shipped

  The array/slice/ownership model (docs/language.md, GRAMMAR.peg, patterns.md
  now document it authoritatively):

  - T[N] — sized value. Owns its storage, bounds-checked, .length is the
  compile-time N.
  - T[] — slice. A {ptr, len} fat pointer (runtime length, no size in
  signatures). Borrowed (xs: T[], read-only) or owned (own xs: T[],
  mutable/movable). Lives at the func boundary; T[N] decays to it; returning a
  slice that traces to a local is rejected (dangling).
  - Move-by-default. move/copy are general value operators (any position). A
  bare move-only name (array/slice or opaque) is an implicit move (consumed) at
  bind/assign/return/own-arg; scalars copy. The editor shows a ghost move inlay
  on every elided transfer (including opaque). Mutating a non-owned buffer
  requires own — no mutable aliasing.

  for-in removed entirely — rejected at parse, all dead range-for code excised
  from lower/semantic/codegen (incl. a stray DEBUG: print), collateral unit
  tests cleaned. Iteration is sys (archetypes) + C-style for.

  How it went, stage by stage

  - A–D: slice fat-pointer ABI (ptr, len), borrowed/own slices, fat-pointer
  return + dangling guard, T[N]→T[] decay. (385→394)
  - E: general move/copy, move-by-default for arrays+opaque, copy clone, a := 
  b/a = b move, elided-move LSP hint, ~5 tests migrated. (→397)

● Good — that surfaces exactly what's possible. Two findings:

  1. Negative literals work now (-1). The router is littered with 0 - 1 (a
  workaround) — ~10 occurrences. Pure readability win, do it regardless.
  2. Sub-slicing arr[lo:hi] is still not available (we deferred it) — and that's
  the feature that actually makes the router clean.

  Here's the honest assessment. The thing that makes resolve ugly is the capture
  buffers + explicit count:

  resolve :: proc(path: char[])(cstart: int[8], clen: int[8], handler_id: int,
  count: int)
  //                                     ^magic size        ^magic size
      ^count carried separately

  The slice model lets me drop the magic [8] (thread own int[] instead), but the
  count can't go away without sub-slicing — because a slice's .length is the
  buffer's capacity (8), not how many spans were written. The clean version
  wants to hand back cstart[:count] — a right-sized view whose .length is the
  count:

  // with sub-slicing — the count disappears, it's just .length:
  resolve :: func(path: char[], own cs: int[], own ls: int[]) -> (int[], int[],
  route) {
    ...
    return cs[:count], ls[:count], handler;     // right-sized slices; caller
  reads cstart.length
  }

  So the real lever is sub-slicing — which the plan called "a trivial later add
  given the (ptr,len) repr" (a slice op just GEPs the ptr and sets a smaller
  len).

  So two paths:

● User declined to answer questions
  ⎿  · How clean do you want the router, given sub-slicing is what removes the 
     explicit `count`? (Add sub-slicing, then full rewrite / Cosmetic cleanup 
     only, now)

❯ explain which part exactly wants sub slicing, couldn't we write som stdlib    
  for splitting arrays instead?                                                 
  ⎿  Interrupted · What should Claude do instead?

❯ functions don't have multi return, that conflicts with the func as value      
  philosophy                                                                    

● Good corrections — both land. Let me re-answer precisely.

  Where sub-slicing was actually wanted

  Exactly one place: to make the count disappear. resolve writes count spans
  into capacity-8 buffers; to hand back a view whose .length is the count, you'd
  need cstart[:count]. That's the only thing sub-slicing buys the router.

  And honestly — it's not essential. Returning count explicitly is perfectly
  clear. So sub-slicing is a "nice fold," not a real need here.

  Could a stdlib function split instead?

  It could, but it wouldn't avoid the hard part. A take(xs: int[], n: int) -> 
  int[] would return {xs.ptr, n} — but the language has no way to construct a 
  slice with a chosen length except the sub-slice operation itself. So a stdlib
  take is just sub-slicing wearing a function's clothes; it still needs the same
  {ptr,len}-builder primitive underneath. Not worth adding for the router.

  You're right about func multi-return

  My func -> (int[], int[], route) sketch was wrong — that breaks func = a value
  (one result). Anything with multiple outputs is a proc with out-params. So
  resolve stays a proc; the spans/handler/count come back as out-params, full
  stop.

  So what does "cleaner now" actually mean?

  Once you keep it a proc with out-params, the capture buffers are out-only 
  sized params — which is our own idiom (the D6 decision: in-out shadow is
  foreign-only; our procs use out-only). The int[8] there is explicit capacity,
  not noise. So the slice model doesn't actually improve the capture mechanism —
  it was already idiomatic.

  The genuine wins the new work unlocked are smaller and real:

  1. 0 - 1 → -1 — negative literals work now; the router uses 0 - 1 ~10 times
  purely as a workaround.
  2. Type the handler as route, not raw int — add(pattern, handler_id: int),
  resolve(…)(…, handler_id: int), and TrieNode.handler_id :: int all carry a
  route value as a bare int. Making them route is the enum-safety the D6 note
  intended but the code didn't finish.

  That's the honest scope: a readability/type-safety pass, no new feature, still
  a proc with out-params. Want me to do those two on both bench_proc and
  bench_func?

✻ Crunched for 1m 16s

❯ okay explain how sub slicing would work and not interfere with the current    
  ownership system                                                              

● Good question — the answer is that sub-slicing doesn't need any new ownership
  machinery, because a sub-slice is just a borrow, and borrows already exist.
  Here's the full picture.

  What xs[lo:hi] produces

  A slice is {ptr, len}. Sub-slicing is pure pointer arithmetic on that pair:

  xs[lo:hi]  →  { xs.ptr + lo, hi - lo }

  No allocation, no copy — a narrower view of the same storage. xs[:hi],
  xs[lo:], xs[:] are the obvious shorthands. Bounds-checked: 0 ≤ lo ≤ hi ≤ 
  xs.length (elidable when provable, same as indexing).

  Why it slots into ownership with zero new rules

  The ownership system has exactly three relationships: own (the storage), move
  (transfer the own), borrow (a read-only view that consumes nothing). A
  sub-slice is the third one — a borrow — so it inherits all its rules for free:

  - Non-consuming. xs[lo:hi] does not move or consume xs (borrows never do). xs
  stays fully alive, and you can take as many sub-slices as you want — they're
  all read-only, so there's no writer to conflict.
  - Read-only. You cannot write through a sub-slice, exactly like any borrowed
  T[]. This is the load-bearing part: even though xs[2:5] overlaps xs, it can't
  mutate it, so there's no mutable-aliasing hazard. Mutation stays where it
  always was — the single owner, whole-buffer.
  - Lifetime. A sub-slice borrows xs's storage, so it can't outlive it. That's
  the rule you already enforce: a returned slice must trace to a buffer passed 
  in; a slice of a local is rejected (dangling). A sub-slice of a param traces
  to the param → fine; a sub-slice of a fresh local → rejected. Nothing new.

  So move/consume/single-writer are completely untouched — a borrow never
  participates in them. The only genuinely new code is the bounds check on lo/hi
  and the ptr+lo GEP.

  The one place it must respect your earlier decision

  You decided slices aren't free-floating locals — they live at the function 
  boundary (param/return), not ys := xs[2:5] bound to a local. That decision is
  why the read/write alias never bites: a borrow only exists for the duration of
  a call, during which the caller is suspended and can't mutate the owner
  underneath it.

  Sub-slicing has to honor the same boundary: it's valid in argument position
  (and as a return tracing to a param), not as a bare local binding. So:

  seg_eq(stored, path[s:e])        // ✓ borrow a sub-range for the call — path
  untouched, caller suspended
  ys := path[s:e]                  // ✗ same rule that forbids slice locals

  That restriction is what guarantees non-interference: a sub-slice can never be
  live while its owner mutates, because it only exists across a call.

  What it actually buys the router

  This is the honest scope. Arg-position sub-slicing cleans up the span-passing:
  today seg_eq(stored: char[], p: char[], start, end) threads raw indices and
  re-derives the range internally; with sub-slices it's seg_eq(stored, 
  path[s:e]) and seg_eq just compares two slices. Same for seg_end. That removes
  the index-arithmetic plumbing.

  It does not cleanly fold the count away — that needed a sub-slice in
  return/out position (cstart[:count]), which collides with both "no func
  multi-return" and the no-slice-locals boundary. So the capture buffers +
  explicit count stay as-is (and that's fine).

  So: sub-slicing is a clean, additive borrow — no new ownership concept, gated
  to the same func-boundary as other slices — and its router payoff is tidier
  span comparisons, not eliminating the count. Want me to add it (arg-position
  read-only sub-slice, bounds-checked) and then rewrite the router's seg helpers
  around it?

  :id=42, then fails to descend into posts/:pid. This is pre-existing (HEAD
  shows h=-1, c=1). The "18000000 checksum" in the docs has been measuring a
  failed resolve all along — both routers agree only because they share the same
  broken traversal.

  The smell: the trie is linked with archetype column writes
  (TrieNode.first_child[cur] = found, TrieNode.next_sibling[...] = ...). If
  those writes don't persist — which is exactly the known

  — the trie is malformed past the first level, so resolve can't find posts
  under :id.

● Fixed and guarded. 396/396 green.

  Diagnosis (your suspicion was exactly right)

  Highlighting is derived from the compiler: cst/token_category.c's
  arche_token_category() maps each token kind to a category
  (keyword/operator/…), and that feeds both arche-analyzer (the LSP/editor) and
  the arche-cst-tokens dumper. The lexer was fine — enum is a proper TOK_ENUM
  keyword token, lexed identically to proc/func/arche.

  The bug was purely in that map: the "keyword" case group listed every keyword
  token except TOK_ENUM and TOK_MATCH — so both fell through to default → NULL
  and rendered as plain text. They were simply never added when the enum/match
  feature landed.

  Fix

  - Added TOK_ENUM and TOK_MATCH to the keyword group.
  - Closed the same-class adjacent gaps I spotted while there: &&/|| (were
  unmapped → now operator) and ... (now punctuation).

  Regression guard

  tests/unit/tooling/keyword_highlight.arche runs arche-cst-tokens and asserts
  enum and match categorize as keyword. It anchors on column (stable) rather
  than line, so it survives comment edits and will catch any future keyword
  silently dropped from the map.

  Verified: enum and match now emit keyword; full suite 396/396.

● 396/396 green. Done — and it's now structurally impossible to add a token and
  forget to highlight it.

  What changed

  1. Exhaustive switch, no default. arche_token_category now lists every
  TokenKind — including the genuinely-uncategorized ones
  (TOK_EOF/TOK_ERROR/TOK_HASH → explicit NULL). Dropping the catch-all default
  is the key: a default is exactly what silently swallowed enum/match.
  2. -Werror=switch on that file. A dedicated Makefile rule compiles
  token_category.o with switch-exhaustiveness as a hard error. Proven above:
  adding TOK_FAKEKW produced error: enumeration value 'TOK_FAKEKW' not handled 
  in switch and failed the build at the exact line to fix.
  3. CI backstop — the keyword_highlight.arche test still guards the actual
  output.

  So the wrong path (add token, ship it uncategorized) no longer compiles; the
  right path (categorize it) is the only way forward. Two layers: the compiler
  stops you first, the test catches it if the compiler somehow doesn't.

  The general principle

  This is "lean on the compiler for exhaustiveness" — an enum switch with a 
  default is a silent trapdoor for every future variant. The reusable pattern,
  now demonstrated and with Makefile machinery in place:

  ▎ Dispatch on an enum → no default; list every case (the no-op ones 
  ▎ explicitly) → compile with -Werror=switch. Adding a variant then can't build
  ▎ until every dispatch site is updated.

  The same trapdoor exists at other registries that dispatch on a kind with a
  default — HIR node kinds, TypeRef/TyKind, diagnostic dispatch. Converting
  those to exhaustive + -Werror=switch (the per-file rule pattern is now
  established) would make "forgot to handle the new variant" a compile error
  across the compiler. I didn't sweep them now (some defaults are legitimate,
  and there are pre-existing -Wswitch gaps to clean first), but that's the path
  to make it broadly "harder to do wrong than right."

  Want me to do that sweep — audit the enum-dispatch sites and convert the ones
  that should be exhaustive?

● Done — the safeguard is now global, not file-specific.

  What changed

  1. -Werror=switch is project-wide (in CFLAGS), and the per-file rule is gone.
  Now every switch over an enum without a default — anywhere in the compiler —
  must be exhaustive, or the build fails. This is "do the same for every
  category" in one stroke: token kinds, type kinds, HIR tags, node kinds, all of
  them.
  2. Fixed the two pre-existing gaps that were blocking a global flip (both
  switches over the type-kind enum missing TYPE_PROC/TYPE_FUNC):
    - cst.c type_ref_free — added the callable case. This was also a real memory
  leak (the param/result TypeRef arrays were never freed), so the
  exhaustiveness check found a latent bug, exactly as intended.
    - semantic.c type_ref_equal — added proper structural comparison for
  callable types.
  3. Updated the token_category.c comment to reflect the project-wide rule.

  Why global is the right call

  You were right that file-specific was arbitrary. The principle — a no-default 
  switch must be exhaustive — isn't special to highlighting; it's how you make
  the compiler enforce "handle the new variant everywhere" for any enum. With it
  global:

  - Adding a TOK_*, a TYPE_*, a HIR_TYPE_*, a node kind, etc. → the build breaks
  at every dispatch site that doesn't handle it, pointing you to each one.
  - A default is still allowed where a partial handler is genuinely intended —
  that's an explicit opt-out, not a silent trapdoor.

  Proven: injecting a fake TOK_FAKEKW now fails with error: enumeration value 
  'TOK_FAKEKW' not handled in switch [-Werror=switch] at the categorizer;
  removing it builds clean. Suite 396/396.

  The general guidance is now enforced by the toolchain: omit the default, list
  every case, and the compiler guarantees you didn't forget one.

      1596 +/* Emit a `buf[lo:hi]` sub-slice. Computes the element pointe
           +r `base.ptr + lo` and runtime length                         
      1597 + * `hi - lo`, writing them into ptr_out / len_out (caller cha
           +r[256]) and the element base name +                          
      1598 + * bit width into *elem_out / *bitw_out. Returns 1 on success
           + (base is a known array/slice), 0 if the                     
      1599 + * base isn't sliceable. Omitted bounds default to lo=0, hi=b
           +ase length. A read-only borrowed view —                      
      1600 + * no copy, no consume. */                                   
      1601 +static int codegen_slice(CodegenContext *ctx, HirExpr *e, cha
           +r *ptr_out, char *len_out, const char **elem_out,            
      1602 +                         int *bitw_out) {                    
      1603 +  HirExpr *base = e->data.slice.base;                        
      1604 +  ValueInfo *bv = (base->kind == HIR_EXPR_NAME) ? find_value(
           +ctx, base->data.name.name) : NULL;                           
      1605 +  if (!bv)                                                   
      1606 +    return 0;                                                
      1607 +  const char *elem_base, *elem_llvm;                         
      1608 +  char base_ptr[256], base_len[64];                          
      1609 +  if (bv->type == 6 && bv->field_type) {                     
      1610 +    elem_base = bv->field_type;                              
      1611 +    elem_llvm = llvm_type_from_arche(elem_base);             
      1612 +    strcpy(base_ptr, bv->llvm_name);                         
      1613 +    if (bv->is_slice && bv->len_ssa)                         
      1614 +      snprintf(base_len, sizeof(base_len), "%s", bv->len_ssa)
           +;                                                            
      1615 +    else                                                     
      1616 +      snprintf(base_len, sizeof(base_len), "%d", bv->string_l
           +en);                                                         
      1617 +  } else if (bv->type == 7) {                                
      1618 +    elem_base = "char";                                      
      1619 +    elem_llvm = "i8";                                        
      1620 +    char *ep = gen_value_name(ctx);                          
      1621 +    buffer_append_fmt(ctx, "  %s = getelementptr [%d x i8], [
           +%d x i8]* %s, i64 0, i64 0\n", ep, bv->string_len,           
      1622 +                      bv->string_len, bv->llvm_name);        
      1623 +    strcpy(base_ptr, ep);                                    
      1624 +    snprintf(base_len, sizeof(base_len), "%d", bv->string_len
           +);                                                           
      1625 +  } else {                                                   
      1626 +    return 0;                                                
      1627 +  }                                                          
      1628 +  char lo64[256] = "0";                                      
      1629 +  if (e->data.slice.lo) {                                    
      1630 +    char b[256];                                             
      1631 +    codegen_expression(ctx, e->data.slice.lo, b);            
      1632 +    emit_index_i64(ctx, b, e->data.slice.lo, lo64);          
      1633 +  }                                                          
      1634 +  char hi64[256];                                            
      1635 +  if (e->data.slice.hi) {                                    
      1636 +    char b[256];                                             
      1637 +    codegen_expression(ctx, e->data.slice.hi, b);            
      1638 +    emit_index_i64(ctx, b, e->data.slice.hi, hi64);          
      1639 +  } else {                                                   
      1640 +    snprintf(hi64, sizeof(hi64), "%s", base_len);            
      1641 +  }                                                          
      1642 +  char *ptr2 = gen_value_name(ctx);                          
      1643 +  buffer_append_fmt(ctx, "  %s = getelementptr %s, %s* %s, i6
           +4 %s\n", ptr2, elem_llvm, elem_llvm, base_ptr, lo64);        
      1644 +  char *len2 = gen_value_name(ctx);                          
      1645 +  buffer_append_fmt(ctx, "  %s = sub i64 %s, %s\n", len2, hi6
           +4, lo64);                                                    
      1646 +  snprintf(ptr_out, 256, "%s", ptr2);                        
      1647 +  snprintf(len_out, 256, "%s", len2);                        
      1648 +  *elem_out = elem_base;                                     
      1649 +  *bitw_out = strcmp(elem_llvm, "double") == 0 ? 64 : (strcmp
           +(elem_llvm, "i8") == 0 ? 8 : 32);                            
      1650 +  return 1;                                                  
      1651 +}                                                            
      1652 +

      1654

            expr, char *result_buf) {
