● Update(parser/parser.c)
  ⎿  Added 33 lines
      1057  
      1058  /* ========== EXPRESSION PARSING ========== */
      1059  
      1060 +/* After a postfix `[` is matched, parse either an index list
           + `a, b, …` or a slice `lo:hi` (lo/hi                         
      1061 + * each optional: `[:hi]`, `[lo:]`, `[:]`). Sets *out_slice=1
           + for the slice form. Consumes the `]`.                       
      1062 + * A `:` inside the brackets is what marks a slice. */       
      1063 +static int parse_bracket_index_or_slice(Parser *parser, int *
           +out_slice) {                                                 
      1064 +  *out_slice = 0;                                            
      1065 +  if (check(parser, TOK_COLON)) { /* [:hi] / [:] — no lo */  
      1066 +    *out_slice = 1;                                          
      1067 +    advance(parser);                                         
      1068 +    if (!check(parser, TOK_RBRACKET))                        
      1069 +      if (!parse_expression(parser))                         
      1070 +        return 0;                                            
      1071 +  } else {                                                   
      1072 +    if (!parse_expression(parser))                           
      1073 +      return 0;                                              
      1074 +    if (check(parser, TOK_COLON)) { /* [lo:hi] / [lo:] */    
      1075 +      *out_slice = 1;                                        
      1076 +      advance(parser);                                       
      1077 +      if (!check(parser, TOK_RBRACKET))                      
      1078 +        if (!parse_expression(parser))                       
      1079 +          return 0;                                          
      1080 +    } else { /* index: optional comma list */                
      1081 +      while (match(parser, TOK_COMMA) && !check(parser, TOK_R
           +BRACKET))                                                    
      1082 +        if (!parse_expression(parser))                       
      1083 +          return 0;                                          
      1084 +    }                                                        
      1085 +  }                                                          
      1086 +  if (!match(parser, TOK_RBRACKET)) {                        
      1087 +    error(parser, "Expected ']'");                           
      1088 +    return 0;                                                
      1089 +  }                                                          
      1090 +  return 1;                                                  
      1091 +}                                                            
      1092 +                                                             
      1093  /* `out_kind` receives the SyntaxNodeKind for the primary exp
            ression form parsed,
      1094   * derived from parse context (not from a built AST node). Th
            e caller wraps the
      1095   * CST node with it. Left untouched when the primary already 
            wrapped itself (paren). */

      3951        }

      3954 +  case SN_SLICE_EXPR: {                                      
      3955 +    /* `base[lo:hi]` — base is IDENT + fields (as for index);

● Sub-slicing is in. 399/399 green, doctests ok.

  What shipped: arr[lo:hi] sub-slicing

  A read-only borrowed sub-view, exactly the design we locked — and it slotted
  into the ownership model with zero new ownership concepts (it's just a
  borrow):

  - Forms: buf[lo:hi], buf[:hi], buf[lo:], buf[:] → {ptr+lo, hi-lo}, no copy, no
  consume.
  - Bounds-checked: 0 ≤ lo ≤ hi ≤ length, aborts otherwise (reuses the OOB
  idiom).
  - Works as a call argument (sum(a[2:5])) and bound to a local (mid := a[2:5],
  mid.length == sub-length).
  - Full pipeline: new SN_SLICE_EXPR / EXPR_SLICE / HIR_EXPR_SLICE, parser,
  CST→AST, lower, semantic resolve, codegen (codegen_slice helper). 3 tests;
  docs (language.md + GRAMMAR.peg) updated.

  The -Werror=switch safeguard paid off immediately — adding the new expr kind
  broke the build at every dispatch site (free, name, rename, codegen) and
  walked me through each one. Exactly "harder to do wrong than right."

  The router cleanup is blocked — by a pre-existing bug, not the slice feature

  Trying char sub-slices for seg_eq surfaced two char-path gaps:
  1. char[] .length is unimplemented — it emits a literal ret i32 length
  (invalid IR!). Nothing hit it before because the router uses strlen. That's a
  real latent bug.
  2. char-slice → char[] param wraps in an arche_array with a placeholder length
  0, so the sub-length is lost.

  So sub-slicing works for int[]/float[]/etc., but char sub-slices can't flow to
  char[] params until those are fixed. The router seg-helper cleanup depends on
  that, so it's a separate piece.

  Two options:
  - Fix the char-path gaps (char[] .length → load the arche_array len;
  char-slice wrap → carry the real len), then do the router seg_eq(stored, 
  path[s:e]) cleanup.
  - Leave the router as-is (it works) and treat char sub-slices as a follow-up.

  Want me to fix the char-path gaps (worth it regardless — char[] .length
  emitting a bogus token is a bug), or stop here with non-char sub-slicing done?

● Done. The router benches now use sub-slicing.

  What changed

  Both bench_proc.arche and bench_func.arche:

  // before: indices threaded in, p[start + i] offset arithmetic
  seg_eq :: func(stored: char[], p: char[], start: int, end: int) -> int { ...
  p[start + i] ... }
  if (seg_eq(cs, path, s, e) == 1) { ... }

  // after: a borrowed sub-slice; the body indexes seg[i] from 0
  seg_eq :: func(stored: char[], seg: char[], n: int) -> int { ... seg[i] ... }
  if (seg_eq(cs, path[s:e], e - s) == 1) { ... }

  The path segment is passed as a char[] sub-slice (path[s:e]), so seg_eq
  indexes seg[i] directly — the start-offset arithmetic is gone, and the call
  site expresses "this segment" as a slice rather than a (buffer, start, end)
  triple.

  Verified

  - Both build and run; checksum 20000000 on both (identical, and unchanged from
  before the slice rewrite — correctness preserved).
  - Perf a wash (proc ~26 / func ~31 ns/op).
  - Suite still 399/399 (benches live in design_analysis/, not run by make 
  test).
  - Updated COMPARISON.md + DECISIONS.md: corrected the stale checksum (18M →
  20M — the 18M predated the earlier route-enum/miss-sentinel change) and
  documented the sub-slice usage.

  One honest caveat (carried in the bench comment + docs)

  seg_eq still takes an explicit length n because char[].length isn't wired for
  the char/arche_array path (it currently emits a bogus token — a real
  pre-existing bug). With it, seg.length would replace n and the call would be
  just seg_eq(cs, path[s:e]). The sub-slice itself works for char (indexing is
  correct, as proven); only the .length read on a char slice is missing.

  So: the genuine slice cleanup is in (no more start-offset arithmetic), and the
  remaining polish (drop the explicit n) is gated on fixing char[].length —
  which is worth doing on its own since it's emitting invalid IR today. Want me
  to fix char[].length next?

✻ Sautéed for 4m 4s
❯ so is there any real difference between the proc and func versions? what else 
  did we need to make the func version actually (more) functional (It will      
  never be completely pure)                                                     

● Honest answer, grounded in what the two files actually are now.

  Is there a real difference?

  One real difference: reentrancy. That's it.

  First, a clarification the names obscure: both resolves are procs. The "func"
  version isn't using func — it's a proc with out-params. The split is purely
  where per-request capture state lives:

  ┌──────────────┬──────────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────┐
  │              │          bench_proc          │        bench_func         │
  ├──────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ capture      │ module globals               │ caller out-params         │
  │ state        │ (cap_start[8], cap_len[8],   │ (cstart, clen, count)     │
  │              │ cap_count)                   │                           │
  ├──────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ reentrant?   │ no — next resolve clobbers   │ yes — each call writes    │
  │              │ the shared slot              │ the caller's own buffers  │
  ├──────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ trie pool    │ global TrieNode[256]         │ global TrieNode[256]      │
  │              │                              │ (same)                    │
  ├──────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ resolution   │ identical                    │ identical                 │
  │ logic        │                              │                           │
  ├──────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ perf         │ ~26 ns/op                    │ ~31 ns/op (≈ wash)        │
  └──────────────┴──────────────────────────────┴───────────────────────────┘

  So the only substantive difference is reentrancy / composability — you can
  nest or run concurrent resolves with the out-param version without corruption;
  the module-static one has a single shared capture slot. Not purity, not
  speed.

  What it would take to be more functional

  You're right it can never be pure — two hard floors: no heap (can't return
  freshly-allocated persistent structures) and the trie is a global archetype 
  pool by design (the data-oriented model is shared mutable state). The trie
  stays global in any version.

  But the capture path can move further along the imperative→functional
  spectrum, and the slice/own/move work we just did is exactly what unlocks the
  next rung:

  - Rung 1 (most imperative): module-global captures — shared, clobbered.
  (bench_proc)
  - Rung 2 (where bench_func is): out-params — caller-owned buffers, reentrant,
  but still mutate-in-place into "write here" slots, with a separate count.
  - Rung 3 (now possible): owned-slice threading. Caller moves an own int[]
  capture buffer in; resolve fills it and hands it back as a slice; the buffer
  is linearly owned (single writer) and value-threaded rather than
  side-effected. The count disappears — it's the returned slice's .length via
  cstart[:count].

  // rung 3 — the captures flow in and back as values, count == .length
  resolve :: proc(path, own starts: int[], own lens: int[])
                       (starts: int[], lens: int[], handler: route) { ... return
  starts[:count] ... }

  The pieces that made rung 3 reachable are precisely what we built: own T[] + 
  move (linear single-owner buffers), slice return (hand the fat pointer back),
  runtime .length (count rides in the slice), and sub-slicing cstart[:count]
  (right-size the result). And these are int slices, so unlike the router's char
  segments, .length already works — no char-path blocker here.

  The remaining gap to actually ship rung 3: a proc returning a sub-length slice
  through an out-param (starts[:count]) — slice return works for
  func-by-own-param today; the proc-out-param-as-sized-slice path needs a little
  wiring.

  So: the difference today is reentrancy; "more functional" means replacing
  in-place out-params with linearly-owned, value-threaded slices (rung 3), which
  the slice model now makes expressible — and that's the honest ceiling,
  because the trie pool and no-heap keep true purity off the table.

  Want me to build the rung-3 version as a third bench (bench_owned.arche) so
  the comparison shows all three?

❯ we can't return persistent, but we do have copy out now, right? so we can     
  return structure that will exist somewhere, right?                            

● Let me verify the exact current state before answering — there's an important
  distinction between copy (which we have) and copy-out return (which I
  deferred):

● Bash(cd /home/curt/Code/arche
      echo "=== 1. return a fresh LOCAL array by value? ==="…)
  ⎿  === 1. return a fresh LOCAL array by value? ===
     Error: cannot return a local array by value (array copy-out is not imp
     lemented); return an `own` parameter or thread a caller-provided buffe
     … +16 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

● Careful — there's a real distinction here, and the premise is half-right:

  What we actually have

  - copy (the operator): yes. b := copy a clones an array — but into a binding
  in the same frame. The clone lives where b lives.
  - Copy-out return: no. Returning a fresh local array by value still errors —
  return b where b is a local gives "array copy-out is not implemented." I
  planned it in Stage E and deferred it. So mk() -> int[4] { b: int[4]; return 
  b; } does not compile yet.

  So we have duplication, not value-return-by-copy.

  But your instinct is exactly right about "exists somewhere"

  The reason returning a local array is hard isn't that the value can't exist —
  it's where. Two non-heap places a returned structure can live:

  1. The caller's frame, via copy-out (sret). The caller reserves the result
  slot before the call; the callee memcpies its local into that slot and
  returns. The structure then lives in the caller's frame, which outlives the
  call — no dangle, no heap. This is real value-return, and it's exactly the
  deferred piece. We already have the memcpy machinery (copy uses it), so wiring
  it is the trailing-sret-pointer + memcpy from the plan.
  2. Caller-provided storage, via threading — which works today. fill(own xs: 
  int[]) -> int[] (verified above: returns 7) — the caller allocated buf, moved
  it in, got it back. The structure "exists somewhere" = the caller's buf.
  You're already returning a structure that persists; the caller just had to
  provide its home.

  So: copy-out and threading are the same idea — the result lives in the
  caller's frame. Threading makes the caller's ownership explicit (own/move);
  copy-out hides it behind a normal-looking return and a hidden sret slot.
  Threading we have; copy-out is the ergonomic sugar we deferred.

  One caveat for the router specifically: copy-out is for fixed-size T[N] (the
  callee memcpies a known N into the caller's slot). The router's captures are
  variable-count in a fixed int[8] — copy-out would hand back the whole int[8]
  plus a separate count, same as out-params. So copy-out doesn't remove the
  count there; it just lets a func look value-returning.

  Want me to implement copy-out return now? It's the last deferred Stage-E
  piece, it's straightforward given the copy memcpy path, and it'd make mk() -> 
  int[N] { … return local } compile — the result landing in the caller's frame,
  exactly the "exists somewhere" you're describing.
